Patrick Njane Mbugua & another [2020] eKLR</p>\n                                            <img class=\"sub_meta\" src=\"https://kenyalaw.org/caselaw/assets/logo.png\" alt=\"\">\n                                            <div id=\"case_meta\">\n                                                <table class=\"meta_info\" >\n                                                    <tr>\n                                                        <td colspan=\"2\" ><h3><span>Case Metadata </span></h3></td>\n                                                    </tr>\n Limited v Patrick Njane Mbugua & William Macharia  </td>  </tr>\n                                                    <tr><th>Date Delivered: </th><td>04 May 2020</td></tr>\n                                                    <tr ><th>Case Class: </th><td>Civil</td></tr>\n                                                    <tr ><th>Court: </th><td>High Court at Nairobi (Milimani Law Courts)</td></tr>\n                                                    <tr ><th>Case Action: </th><td>Judgment</td></tr>\n                                                    <tr><th>Judge(s): </th><td>Anthony Ndung'u Kimani</td></tr>\n                                                    <tr><th>Citation:</th> <td>Kenya Power And Lighting Company Limited v Patrick Njane Mbugua & another    </div>\n\n                                            <!-- <p class=\"sub_meta\">REPUBLIC OF KENYA</p>-->\n                                            <div class=\"case_number sub_meta\"></div>\n                                            <div class=\"case_content\" id=\"case_content\"><p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>REPUBLIC OF KENYA</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>IN THE HIGH COURT OF KENYA</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>MILIMANI LAW COURTS</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>CIVIL APPEAL DIVISION</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>CIVIL APPEAL NO 232 OF 2016</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong>KENYA POWER AND LIGHTING COMPANY LIMITED......................APPELLANT</strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong>VERSUS</strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong>PATRICK NJANE MBUGUA.............................................................1<sup>ST</sup> RESPONDENT</strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong>WILLIAM MACHARIA....................................................................2<sup>ND</sup> RESPONDENT</strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><em>(Being an appeal against the Judgment and Decree of Hon. Onsarigo (RM) </em></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><em>delivered on 27<sup>th</sup> January 2016 in Thika CMCC No. 1219 of 2010)</em></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: center;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>JUDGEMENT</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>INTRODUCTION</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">1. This is an appeal against the judgment of Hon. Onsarigo R.M dated 27<sup>th</sup> January 2016. The 1<sup>st</sup> respondent instituted a claim in the lower court against the appellant seeking general and special damages for injuries sustained in a road traffic accident. The 1<sup>st</sup> respondent claimed that on 25<sup>th</sup> November 2007 the appellant&rsquo;s motor vehicle KMT 853 was being driven by the appellant&rsquo;s driver or agent so negligently al<u>ong Nairobi-Thika road near Theta club</u>that it lost control and rolled several times. The 1<sup>st</sup> respondent was a passenger in the vehicle and he claims that as a result of the accident he sustained serious injuries. The appellant filed itsdefense denying the claim and also averred that it was not the owner of motor vehicle KMT 853 as at 25<sup>th</sup> November 2007. In due course the defendant issued a third party notice to the 2<sup>nd </sup>Respondent claiming that the 2<sup>nd</sup> respondent was the owner of the vehicle at the time of the accident.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">2. Before considering the facts and the parties&rsquo; submissions I must first recognize that the duty of the first appellate court is to re-evaluate and re-assess the evidence adduced before the trial court keeping in mind that the trial court saw and heard the parties and giving allowance for that and to reach an independent conclusion as to whether to uphold the judgment (see&nbsp;<em><strong>Selle v Associated Motor Boat Co.</strong></em><strong>&nbsp;[1968] EA 123</strong>).</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">3. Patrick NjaneMbugua (Pw1) recalled that on the material day he was a passenger in motor vehicle KTM 853. He testified that when they got to Kenyatta road the driver was driving at an abnormal speed and lost control. He became unconscious and was taken to the Hospital by good Samaritans. He was later referred to Thika Level 5 Hospital and thereafter to a private hospital. He testified that he suffered a fracture on his right leg and arm and also had a dislocated arm. He reported the incident to Ruiru police station</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">4. Dr. Cyprus Oketch Okere (Pw2) testified that the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent sustained bruises on the nose, blunt back injury, dislocation of the right shoulder and comminuted segmental fracture of the right femur.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">5. The appellant called Jerald Kihui&nbsp; Karanja<strong>(DW1)</strong> as its witness. <strong>DW1</strong> told court that the vehicle was owned by Kenya Power but was disposed in 1995 to Mrs. Waiganjo at Kshs 75,000/-. He produced the appellant&rsquo;s filed list of documents in support of his testimony.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">6. After conducting an elaborate hearing, the trial magistrate found the appellant 100% liable for the accident, awarded the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent Kshs 800,000/- as general damages and Kshs 63,860/- as special damages. In arriving at its determination the trial court held as follows;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>&ldquo;The plaintiff in his examination in chief did produce a copy of records indicating that as at 25<sup>th</sup> November 2007 the motor vehicle KTM 853 was owned by the Defendant. The defendant on the other hand called one witness who testified that as at 25<sup>th</sup> November 2007 the motor vehicle had been sold to Mrs. Waiganjo. However, no evidence was led to indicate that the third party was the owner of motor vehicle KMT 853 at the time of the accident. The plaintiff did produce a copy of records indicating that the motor vehicle at the time of the accident (sic). I therefore hold that the plaintiff discharged his burden of proof as this was the acceptable way to confirm motor vehicle ownership. I hold that the defendant is vicariously liable and hold 100%liable.&rdquo;</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">7. Dissatisfied with the holding of the trial court, the appellant has filed this instant appeal on the following grounds:</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>1. The learned magistrate erred in law and in fact in failing to properly consider and evaluate the evidence before him which showed that;</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 80px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>a) The appellant was not the beneficial owner of motor vehicle registration number KTM 853.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 80px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>b) That the appellant had sold the said motor vehicle to its employee who later sold it to the third party.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 80px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>c) The appellant had rebutted the presumption of ownership against it.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 80px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>d) The appellant was not in possession and/or control of the vehicle at the time of the accident.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>2. The learned magistrate failed to consider the evidence of the first respondent which confirmed that the owner of the motor vehicle registration number KTM 853 was the third party.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>3. The trial magistrate erred in law in holding that the appellant was vicariously liable for the action of the driver of motor vehicle registration number KTM 853 was the third party.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>4. The trial magistrate erred in law and in fact in failing to consider the appellant&rsquo;s documents produced in evidence.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>5. The learned magistrate erred in law and in fact in failing to properly consider and evaluate the appellant&rsquo;s submissions dated and filed on 11<sup>th</sup> September 2015.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>6. The learned magistrate erred in fact in failing to find that the appellant had proved its defense on a balance of probabilities.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>7. Without prejudice to the foregoing, the award of damages in the any event was manifestly excessive.</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>SUBMISSIONS</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">8. The appeal was dispensed by way of written submissions.Counsel for the appellant, argued in his written submissions that the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent had failed to discharge his burden of proof regarding the ownership of the vehicle. He submitted that the documentary evidence tendered by the appellant had established that it was not the owner of the ill-fated vehicle as it had been sold 15 years prior to the accident. The cases of <strong><em>Nancy Ayemba Ngaira vs Abdi [2010] eKLR, Ignatius Makau Mutisya v Rueben Musyoki Muli [2015] eKLR </em></strong>and<strong><em> Kyoga Hauliers Limited v Malindi Parcels Service &amp; Another [2018] eKLR </em></strong>were cited in support of the appellant&rsquo;s case.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">9. Counsel argued that if the trial magistrate had carefully evaluated the evidence he would have reached the conclusion that the appellant was not the beneficial owner of the vehicle and that it was the third party who was liable for the accident. He pointed out that the third party had not alluded to the relationship of the appellant and the driver and had not denied ownership of the vehicle. He had also conveniently failed to testify.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">10. Counsel also argued that the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent had failed to establish the existence of a contractual or agency relationship between the appellant and the driver of the motor vehicle at the time of the accident; therefore, the trial court had erred in finding that the appellant was vicariously liable for the actions of the driver. Counsel observed that the identity of the driver was unknown to both the appellant and the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent. He surmised that the third party would have identified the driver if he had testified.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">11. The appellant also challenged the trial court&rsquo;s award of damages. Counsel argued that the sum of Kshs. 800,000/= awarded as general damages was excessive as the only serious injury the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent had suffered was a fracture of the femur. He submitted that a sum of Kshs. 500,000/= would have been sufficient compensation for the injuries suffered.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">12. Counsel for the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent countered that the appellant had squandered its opportunity to scrutinize and interrogate the third party and adduce viable evidence to prove that although it was the registered owner, it was the third party who was in possession at the material time. Counsel argued that if the appellant had proved that the third party was in possession, liability would still not move from the registered owner, but it became a shared one with that of the beneficial owner. He contended that the appellant&rsquo;s stature was not the kind that would sell a vehicle to a third party without an agreement. That the appellant also had a sophisticated way of disposing off its property and that if there was a mistake on the side of the appellant, it could not be allowed to take advantage of its own mistake.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">13. On the award of damages, counsel argued that the award of special damages had not been challenged and that the award of general damages was in fact lower than the awards made in the authorities adduced since the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent had suffered a compounded fracture which was a very severe.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">14. Counsel also questioned the jurisdiction of this court to hear the matter given that there had been established High Courts in Thika, Kiambu and Murang&rsquo;a. He also declined to respond to the issues of negligence and vicarious liability on the ground that the issues had not been raised in the memorandum of appeal.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong><u>ANALYSIS AND DETERMINATION</u></strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">15. Before I determine the substantive issues herein, I will first deal with the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent&rsquo;s contention that this court lacked territorial jurisdiction to hear and determine the appeal. The 1<sup>st</sup> respondent&rsquo;s counsel urged this court to find that it did not have jurisdiction to hear the matter since there had been established a High Court at Thika, Kiambu and Murang&rsquo;a. <strong>Order 47 Rule 10</strong> of the <strong>Civil ProcedureRules</strong> provides that an appeal from a decree or order of a subordinate court to the High Court may be filed in the District Registry within the area of which such subordinate court is situated. But then what is the jurisdiction of the High Court?</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">16. The jurisdiction of the High Court is clearly spelt out under Article 165(3) of the Constitution. Through administrative practice, the Chie Justice from time to time by notice in the official Kenya Gazette may amend the schedule listing district registries of the High Court indicating where particular matters may be filed. This may appear to limit the territorial jurisdiction of the High Court. But the converse is true.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">17. The jurisdiction of a court is a donation of the law and cannot be conferred by the parties or through an administrative notice. The High Court in particular derives its jurisdiction, first and foremost, from the constitution and then from any other written law. That jurisdiction may only be limited or ousted altogether by the law.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">18. Unlike the subordinate courts whose territorial jurisdiction is defined by sections 12, 13, 14 and 15 of the Civil Procedures Act, there are no similar substantive provisions in respect of the High Court(See <strong>Riddles burger and Another vs Robinson and Others, 1958 EA 375</strong> which was cited with approval in <strong>Francis Ndichu Gathongo vs Evans Kitazi Ondansa and Another, Civil Appeal No. 287 of 2002)</strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">19. The Court of Appeal <strong>in Sally Njambi Mahihu and Another Vs Mwanguza Kai Deche and Another, [2017] eKLR,</strong> had this to say on the matter;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; &ldquo;<em>&hellip;&hellip;&hellip;&hellip; that the High Court of Kenya remains one and the same court only that it seats at different locations in the country such as Malindi and Nairobi. The location where it seats cannot therefore affect its jurisdiction. The practice and requirement that suits be found in particular stations of the High Court are purely for administration and convenience in the hearing and determination of suits. That is not in any way to suggest that such requirements or practice is unreasonable or unnecessary; it is intended to reduce costs of transporting witnesses from one corner of the country to another for hearing of cases and to expedite hearing and determination of suits, thus giving meaning to the overriding objective and constitutional value in Article 159 which emphasizes the need to reduce costs and delay in the hearing and determination of suits.&rdquo;</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">20. The need to have orderly proceedings in the High Court both in the exercise of its original or appellate jurisdiction cannot be overemphasized. There is a discernable requirement to prevent abuse of the court process by forum shoppers who would hop from a district registry to the next for reasons other than seeking fair audience before the courts. Parties must be cushioned against unnecessary costs and inconvenience.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">21. An issue on jurisdiction ought to be raised at the earliest stage of the proceedings as it is central to the propriety of the entire proceedings. In our instance suit, had this issue been raised at a preliminary point, the court would have had the opportunity to transfer this appeal to the desired registry.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">22. As observed above the jurisdiction of the High Court is not limited. The Appeal has now been heard to conclusion. This court had jurisdiction. There is absolutely no demonstration of any prejudice on the part of the 1<sup>st</sup> Respondent. The objection based on the jurisdiction is dismissed.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">23. Having considered this appeal and the rival submissions by the parties, I found that the main issue raised in the appeal pertains to ownership of motor vehicle registration number KTM 853.In the case of <strong><u>Kabir Mohamed Farouk v Postal Corporation of Kenya [2018] eKLR </u></strong>the court cited the case of&nbsp;<strong><em>Jotham Mugalo Vs. Telkom (K) Ltd Kisumu HCCC No. 166 Of 2001</em></strong>&nbsp;<strong>where </strong>Warsame J held as follows;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>&ldquo;Whereas it is true that it is the responsibility of the plaintiff to prove that the motor vehicle which caused the accident belonged to the defendant and the production of a certificate of search is a valid way of showing the ownership, it is not the only way to show that a particular individual is the owner of the motor vehicle as this can be proved by a police abstract. Since a police abstract is a public document, it is incumbent upon the person disputing its contents to produce such evidence since in a civil dispute the standard of proof requires only balance of probabilities. Where the defendant alleges that the motor vehicle which caused the accident did not belong to him, it is up to them to substantiate that serious allegation by bringing evidence contradicting the documentary evidence produced by the plaintiff as required by section 106 and 107 of the Evidence Act. The particulars of denial contained in the defense cannot be a basis to reject a claim simply because a party has denied the existence of a fact as a fact denied becomes disputed and the dispute can only be resolved on the quality or availability of evidence.&rdquo;</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">24. At the hearing of the matter before the trial court, the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent produced a copy of records indicating that the appellant was the owner of the vehicle. Since the appellant was disputing ownership of the vehicle, the burden of proof shifted to the appellant in accordance with <strong>Section 109</strong> of the <strong>Evidence Act</strong> which provides that, &ldquo;<em>the burden of proof as to any particular fact lies on the person who wishes the court to believe in its existence...&rdquo;</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">25. To discharge that burden, the appellant called <strong>DW1 </strong>who testified that the vehicle had been sold off by the appellant by the time the accident occurred. The witness produced a copy of an internal memorandum of the appellant dated 11<sup>th</sup> January 1995 authorizing the release of the subject vehicle to one Mrs. Waiganjo who had purchased the vehicle for Kshs. 75,000/=. <strong>DW1</strong> also produced a copy of a receipt issued to the said Mrs. Waiganjo for Kshs. 75,000/= as well as a copy of transfer of ownership of the motor vehicle to her.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">26. As held in the cases of <strong><em>Kabir Mohamed Farouk (supra), Nancy Ayemba Ngaira vs Abdi Ali (supra),</em></strong><strong><em> Ignatius Makau Mutisya v Rueben Musyoki Muli (supra) </em></strong>and<strong><em> Kyoga Hauliers Limited v Malindi Parcels Service &amp; Another (supra) </em></strong>the copy of records obtained from the registrar of motor vehicle is not conclusive proof that the sole owner of a vehicle is the person whose name is indicated thereon. Often times, the certificate of records may show that one is the registered owner of a vehicle while its actual possession has passed to a third party. The unchallenged evidence of <strong>DW1</strong> showed that this was the case in the instant suit.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">27. Although the copy of records produced by the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent showed that the appellant was the registered owner, it is my finding that the evidence of <strong>DW1 </strong>adequately disproved the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent&rsquo;s assertion that the appellant was the owner of the vehicle at the material time since his testimony that the appellant had sold the vehicle was not challenged.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">28. This is so because on cross examination the 1<sup>st</sup> Respondent admitted that the police abstract in respect of the accident indicated that the owner of the subject motor vehicle was <strong>William Macharia Chege, the 2<sup>nd</sup> Respondent</strong>. At page 7 of the proceedings, he stated during close examination;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong>&nbsp;&ldquo;</strong><em>&hellip;.. after 3 days I did another search and I noted that the vehicle belonged to William Macharia Chege&rdquo;</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">29. The 1<sup>st</sup> Respondent&rsquo;s counsel was, in cross examination of <strong>DW1</strong>, content with a confirmation that <strong>DW1</strong> had no search certificate to indicate that the motor vehicle belonged to Mrs. Waiganjo. Yet <strong>DW1</strong> had produced compelling evidence to demonstrate that the motor vehicle had left the hands of the appellant and ownership assumed by one Mrs. Waiganjo in 1995. The appellant had by this demonstration discharged its burden of proof and the plaintiff had the onus to prove the ownership of the motor vehicle as at the time of the accident, a task that was glossed over casually obviously with disastrous effect to the 1<sup>st</sup> Respondent&rsquo;s case against the appellant.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">30. Had the trial magistrate addressed his mind to this evidence, he would have more probably than not, arrived at a different finding on liability.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">31. In as much as the appellant failed to lead evidence to show that the vehicle had subsequently been sold to the third party, the evidence on record proved that the third party was the owner of the vehicle at the material time.&nbsp; This position is informed by the fact that the police abstract produced by the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent identified the third party as the owner of the vehicle.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">32. An important aspect of the evidence that in my view was overlooked by the trial court was the position taken by the 2<sup>nd</sup> Respondent in the trial.&nbsp; When it was the turn of the 2<sup>nd</sup> Respondent to testify, counsel for the 2<sup>nd</sup> Respondent stated;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>&ldquo;I will not call any witness. I wish to close the 3<sup>rd</sup> party&rsquo;s case.&rdquo;</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">33. Thus despite the police abstract clearly indicating that the vehicle belonged to the 2<sup>nd</sup> Respondent, the 2<sup>ND</sup> Respondent found no need to explain away why the police abstract, a public official document, would have his name as the owner of the subject motor vehicle if that was not the factual position. The 2<sup>nd</sup> Respondent&rsquo;s filed defence therefore, remained what it is, mere allegations. By letting this opportunity slip through his fingers, the 2<sup>nd </sup>Respondent unwittingly gave credence to the evidence of the appellant to the effect that as at the time of the accident, the appellant was not the owner of the subject motor vehicle.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">34. I find useful guidance in the case of <strong><em>Joel Muga Opinja v East Africa Sea Food Ltd Civil Appeal No. 309 of 2010 [2013] eKLR</em></strong> where the Court of Appeal held;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>&ldquo;We agree that the best way to prove ownership would be to produce to the court a document from the Registrar of Motor vehicles showing who the registered owner is, <u>but when the abstract is not challenged and is produced in court without any objection, its contents cannot be later denied.</u>&rdquo; [Emphasis added]</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">35. In the case of <strong><em>Agro-Chemical Food Company Limited v Joel Angana&amp; 2 others Civil Appeal No. 154, 155 &amp; 156 of 2012 [2016] eKLR</em></strong>which falls on all fours with the present case, Majanja J. held as follows;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><em>&ldquo;14. As the plaintiffs established prima facie evidence that the defendant was the registered owner of the motor vehicle and it was then the duty of the defendant to rebut this presumption on the balance of probabilities. <strong>DW1</strong> produced copies of newspaper advertisements showing that the motor vehicle was advertised for sale through tender on 26th April 1996. The copies of the advertisements produced were certified copies produced without objection and there is no reason to believe that they were not what they purported to be as they appeared regular on the face. Although the defendant failed to produce a sale agreement of transfer of the motor vehicle in favor of Tridev, there is additional evidence to show that the vehicle had indeed transferred on the date of the accident. PW 4 confirmed that the insurance sticker showed that the vehicle was owned by Tridev and the police abstract confirmed as much. I am aware and take judicial notice of the fact that it is mandatory for every motor vehicle owner to take out third party insurance under the Insurance (Motor Vehicle) Third Party Risks Act (Chapter 405 of the Laws of Kenya). Why would Tridev take out insurance if it was not the owner of the motor vehicle? This evidence just goes to show that it is more probable than not that defendant had sold the vehicle to Tridev and it was therefore not the owner on the date of the accident. Had the learned magistrate considered this evidence, he would probably have come to a different conclusion. I therefore find and hold that the defendant had discharged its burden of proving that it was not the owner of the motor vehicle hence the it could not be held liable for what befell the plaintiffs.&rdquo;</em></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">36. The third party, who is the 2<sup>nd</sup> respondent in this appeal, was similarlyidentified as the owner of the vehicle and was also shown to have taken out an insurance cover for the vehicle. The records show that the third party entered appearance and filed a defense as well as a witness statement before the trial court. The court gave directions that all issues arising from the Third Party Notice be dealt with at the hearing of the plaintiff&rsquo;s claim. The Third Party was ably represented during the course of the trial but chose not to testify or call evidence to rebut the claim that he was the owner of the vehicle.I therefore accept the appellant&rsquo;s invitation to draw an adverse inference from his failure to do so.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">37. It is also noteworthy that the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent admitted during cross examination that he had done another search which showed that the vehicle belonged to the third party. It is obvious that based on the evidence on record, the trial magistrate fell into error when making a determination on liability between the three parties.Had the trial magistrate properly considered all facts he would have reached the conclusion, as I have, that the evidence proved that the third party was the owner of the vehicle at the time of the accident.Consequently, he was vicariously liable for the actions of the driver of the vehicle who caused the accident.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">38. As for the award of damages, I am guided by the principle that an appellate court will only interfere with the findings of a trial court on an award of damages if it can be shown that the court proceeded on wrong principles, or misapprehended the evidence in some material respect, and so arrived at a figure which was either inordinately high or low (see <strong><em>Butt v Khan </em><em>[1981] KLR 349</em></strong>).</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">39. There was no dispute that the plaintiff had suffered a dislocation of the right shoulder, a comminuted segmental fracture of the right femur and bruises to the nose and a blunt injury on his lower back. In coming to the conclusion that an award of Kshs. 800,000/= would be adequate compensation for the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent, the trial magistrate properly assessed the authorities adduced by the parties. He also took into account the severity of the 1<sup>st</sup> respondent&rsquo;s injuries and the degree of permanent incapacity which had been assessed at 20%. I find no reason to interfere with the trial court&rsquo;s award of general damages and uphold the award of Kshs. 800,000/= made for general damages.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">40. For special damages, I will reduce the award to Kshs. 46,710/= which was the sum pleaded and proved.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">41. In the end, I find that this appeal is merited and allow it as follows;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">a. The trial court&rsquo;s finding on liability is set aside and substituted with liability at 100% against the 2<sup>nd</sup> respondent;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">b. The trial court&rsquo;s award of general damages is upheld;</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">c. The trial court&rsquo;s award of special damages is set aside and substituted with an award of Kshs. 46,710/=</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"margin-left: 40px; text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\">d. The appellant shall have the costs of the appeal and at the trial court to be borne by the respondents.</span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong>Dated, Signed and delivered at Kisii this 4<sup>th</sup> &nbsp;day of&nbsp; May, 2020.</strong></span></span></p>\n<p style=\"text-align: justify;\">\n\t<span style=\"font-family:times new roman,times,serif;\"><span style=\"font-size:12px;\"><strong>A. K.                              <div class=\"judgement\">\n                                        <div id=\"case_content\">\n\n                                            <p class=\"sub_meta\">Matulai v    </div>\n\n                                            <!-- <p class=\"sub_meta\">REPUBLIC OF KENYA</p>-->\n                                            <div class=\"case_number sub_meta\"></div>\n                                            <div class=\"case_content\" id=\"case_content\"><div class=\"akoma-ntoso\"><span class=\"akn-akomaNtoso\"><article class=\"akn-judgment\" data-name=\"judgment\"><div id=\"header\" class=\"akn-header\">\n  <div class=\"akn-div tausi-header\">\n    <div class=\"akn-div doc-title\"><span class=\"akn-docTitle\">Matulai v Mwikya (Civil Appeal 12 of 2022) [2024] KEHC 1909 (KLR) (22 February 2024) (Judgment)</span></div>\n    \n    \n    <div class=\"akn-div neutral-citation\">\n      Neutral citation:\n      [2024] KEHC 1909 (KLR)\n    </div>\n    \n    \n      <div class=\"akn-div republic-head\">\n        \n          Republic of Kenya\n        \n      </div>\n      <div class=\"akn-div doc-authority\"><span class=\"akn-docAuthority\">In the High Court at Kitui</span></div>\n      \n      <div class=\"akn-div docket-number\"><span class=\"akn-docketNumber\">Civil Appeal 12 of 2022</span></div>\n      \n        <div class=\"akn-div judges\">RK Limo, J</div>\n      \n      <div class=\"akn-div doc-date\">\n        <span class=\"akn-docDate\" data-date=\"2024-02-22\">\n          February 22, 2024\n        </span>\n      </div>\n      \n    \n    \n      \n\n        \n        <div class=\"akn-div parties-listing\">\n          \n            \n              <div class=\"akn-div parties-separator\">Between</div>\n            \n          \n            \n              <div class=\"akn-div party-listing\">\n                <div class=\"akn-div party-name\">Gabriel Silla Matulai</div>\n                <div class=\"akn-div dotted\">  </div>\n                <div class=\"akn-div\">Appellant</div>\n              </div>\n            \n          \n            \n              <div class=\"akn-div parties-separator\">and</div>\n            \n          \n            \n              <div class=\"akn-div party-listing\">\n                <div class=\"akn-div party-name\">Joseph Mwimi Mwikya</div>\n                <div class=\"akn-div dotted\">  </div>\n                <div class=\"akn-div\">Respondent</div>\n              </div>\n            \n          \n        </div>\n      \n    \n    \n      <div class=\"akn-div header-note\">\n        \n          (This is an Appeal from the Judgment of Senior Resident Magistrate, Hon J. W. Wang’anga delivered on 8th June 2022 in Mutomo PMCC NO. E007 OF 2021.)\n        \n      </div>\n    \n    \n  </div>\n<div class=\"akn-div introduction\">\n  \n    <div class=\"akn-div decision-action\">Judgment</div>\n  \n</div>\n</div>\n<div id=\"judgmentBody\" class=\"akn-judgmentBody\"><div id=\"arguments\" class=\"akn-arguments\">\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_1\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">1.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_1__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_1__p_1\">This is an Appeal from the Judgment of Senior Resident Magistrate, Hon J. W. Wang’anga delivered on 8<sup class=\"akn-sup\">th</sup> June 2022 in Mutomo PMCC NO. E007 OF 2021.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_2\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">2.</span><span class=\"akn-intro\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_2__intro__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__intro__p_1\">The cause of action arose from a traffic road accident which occurred on 15<sup class=\"akn-sup\">th</sup> April 2018 along Mutomo-Ikutha road at Uvai area involving motor vehicle registration no. KBM 812B in which it is alleged that the Appellant was travelling in as a passenger. The Appellant averred that the Respondent and/or his authorized driver recklessly and carelessly drove the said motor vehicle causing it to hit the rear of motor vehicle registration KCN 074Y and as a consequence he sustained severe injuries.  The Appellant particularized the injuries sustained as follows;</span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_a\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_a\"><span class=\"akn-num\">a)</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_a__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_a__p_1\">Blunt injury to the chest</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_a_2\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_a_2\"><span class=\"akn-num\">a.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_a_2__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_a_2__p_1\">Blunt injury to the lower back</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_b\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_b\"><span class=\"akn-num\">b.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_b__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_b__p_1\">Blunt injury to the knees</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_c\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_c\"><span class=\"akn-num\">c.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_c__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_2__subpara_c__p_1\">Blunt injury to the hip region.</span></span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_3\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_3\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">3.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_3__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_3__p_1\">In response, the Respondent filed a Defence dated 6<sup class=\"akn-sup\">th</sup> October 2019 where he denied ownership of motor vehicle registration No. KBM 812B, occurrence of the accident and particulars of negligence attribute to him.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_4\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_4\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">4.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_4__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_4__p_1\">The trial court evaluated the evidence tendered and found that the appellant had not 100% proved his case to the required standard and dismissed it. Below is a summary of the evidence tendered at the trial court.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_5\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_5\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">5.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_5__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_5__p_1\">Gabriel Sila Mutulai (PW1) adopted his witness statement dated 10/2/2021 and told the court that he was involved in a traffic road accident on 15/4/2018 when travelling from Kitui to Mutomo. He stated that he was seated next to the driver and that he warned him of over speeding but the driver ignored. He proceeded that the driver was chasing another motor vehicle when he overtook from the left side and caused the accident. He stated that their vehicle was hit because of overtaking. He proceeded that he was taken to a government hospital where he was admitted for two days and thereafter went to Mombasa from where he started experiencing pains on his chest, back and left knee and went to hospital on 17<sup class=\"akn-sup\">th</sup> April 2018. He stated that he reported the matter at Mutomo Police Station. He tendered documents as follows; treatment chit, P3 Form, Police Abstract, NTSC invoice, NTSA motor vehicle copy of records, medical report and referral letter from the police as P ex1 to 7 respectively.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_6\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_6\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">6.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_6__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_6__p_1\">The Respondent on his part presented no witness in his defence.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_7\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_7\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">7.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_7__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_7__p_1\">The trial court as observed above found that the appellant had not proved his case and that the medical document tendered were suspicious and dismissed the case with costs to the respondent.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_8\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">8.</span><span class=\"akn-intro\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_8__intro__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__intro__p_1\">The appellant felt aggrieved and filed this appeal raising the following grounds namely;</span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_i\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_i\"><span class=\"akn-num\">i.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_i__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_i__p_1\">The Learned Magistrate erred in fact and in law in dismissing the Appellant’s suit.</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_ii\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_ii\"><span class=\"akn-num\">ii.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_ii__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_ii__p_1\">The Learned Magistrate erred in fact and in law in finding that the Appellant has not proved his case against the Respondent on a balance of probabilities.</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_iii\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_iii\"><span class=\"akn-num\">iii.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_iii__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_iii__p_1\">The Learned Magistrate erred in fact and in law in finding that the Appellant had not proved his case against the Respondent on a balance of probabilities.</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_iv\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_iv\"><span class=\"akn-num\">iv.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_iv__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_iv__p_1\">The Learned Magistrate erred in fact and in law in failing to consider the plaintiff’s documents on record more so the treatment records.</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_v\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_v\"><span class=\"akn-num\">v.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_v__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_8__subpara_v__p_1\">The Learned Magistrate erred in fact and in law by recording proceedings which was not the Appellant’s testimony more so on the issue of medication being sought at a Mombasa hospital.</span></span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_9\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_9\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">9.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_9__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_9__p_1\">The appellant seeks to set aside the dismissal order and have asked the court to award damages.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_10\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_10\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">10.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_10__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_10__p_1\">The appellant submits that the trial court fell into error in its decision because his case was not challenged during trial. He maintains that the respondent was vicariously liable for the negligent acts of his driver.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_11\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_11\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">11.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_11__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_11__p_1\">Counsel for the appellant cited the case of Counsel has cited the case of Motex Knitwear Mills Limited vs Gopitex Knitwear Mills Limited [2009] eKLR where the court referred to the case of A<a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2014/7990\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2014/7990\">utar Singh &amp; Anor versus Raju Govindji</a> HCC 548 of 1990 where it was found that failure to call evidence renders a party’s pleadings as mere statements and renders evidence adduced by a plaintiff as uncontroverted evidence. Similar finding was arrived at in the case of <a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2014/7990\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2014/7990\">Dorcas Wangithi Nderi vs Samuel Kiburu Mwaura &amp; Anor</a> (2015) Eklr.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_12\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_12\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">12.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_12__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_12__p_1\">On the award for general damages, the Appellant submits that an award of Kshs 400,000/- would be sufficient and prayed for an award of Kshs 3,550/- as Special damages.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_13\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_13\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">13.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_13__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_13__p_1\">This appeal is unopposed but that notwithstanding, the primary role of this court being a first appeal is to reconsider the evidence tendered and draw own conclusions.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_14\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_14\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">14.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_14__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_14__p_1\">On liability, the burden of proof is always on the plaintiff. The trial court was not persuaded that the appellant discharged that burden because it found the appellants case “sketch, shaky and lacking in detail on how the accident occurred”. The trial court found that it was not enough to produce a police abstract to prove negligence on the part of the respondent. While it is true that the burden of proof lies on whoever alleges, it should not be lost that the standard of proof required is on a balance of probabilities.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_15\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_15\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">15.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_15__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_15__p_1\">The Court of Appeal’s position in <a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2014/7990\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2014/7990\">Daniel Toroitich Arap Moi –vs- Mwangi Stephen Muriithi &amp; Another</a> [2014] eKLR espouses the correct legal position that:</span><span class=\"akn-block\" data-name=\"quote\" id=\"arguments__para_15__block_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_15__block_1\"><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure\" data-startQuote=\"“\" id=\"arguments__para_15__block_1__embeddedStructure_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_15__block_1__embeddedStructure_1\"><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure--startQuote\">“</span><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure--content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_15__block_1__embeddedStructure_1__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_15__block_1__embeddedStructure_1__p_1\">It is a firmly settled procedure that even where a defendant has not denied the claim by filing a defence or an affidavit or even where the defendant did not appear, formal proof proceedings are conducted. The claimant lays on the table evidence of facts contended against the defendant. And the trial court has a duty to examine that evidence to satisfy itself that indeed the claim has been proved. If the evidence falls short of the required standard of proof, the claim is and must be dismissed. The standard of proof in a civil case, on a balance of probabilities, does not change even in the absence of rebuttal by the other side.”</span></span></span></span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_16\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_16\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">16.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_16__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_16__p_1\">The burden of proof in this matter was on the Appellant and his pleadings and oral testimony in court was that he was aboard a public service vehicle and he blamed the driver of said motor vehicle for the accident by trying to overtake when it was not safe to do so. His testimony was to the effect that he was travelling aboard the suit motor vehicle, that the same was being driven at a high speed and that the driver was chasing another vehicle, that the driver upon reaching an area called Uvai, the driver tried to overtake on the wrong side (left) and caused the accident.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_17\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_17\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">17.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_17__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_17__p_1\">The burden of proof shifted to the Respondent to rebut these allegations. However, the Respondent did not call any witnesses to rebut evidence tendered. In its judgment, the trial court stated that the Respondent denied accusations raised by the Appellant in its defence and called for the dismissal of the Appellant’s case before closing his case but the record shows that counsel for the Respondent sought for a date for hearing of the defence case on 13/4/2022 but on the same day at a later time chose to close her case without calling any witnesses.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_18\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_18\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">18.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_18__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_18__p_1\">Still on liability, the trial court failed to find in favour of the Appellant for the reasons that he failed to call the investigating officer or tender sketch maps of the scene. She further found that the accident was still under investigations and for the reason that the Appellant experienced difficulty in procuring a police abstract. I have noted some anomalies here. Firstly, the accident was reported at Mutomo Police Station and the police issued an abstract which was tendered in evidence without an objection. Secondly, a police officer if one was called would have provided secondary evidence as they did not eye witnesses to the accident. Thirdly, the appellant cannot be faulted for experiencing difficulty in procuring a police abstract, because the same in my view was beyond his control. Fourthly, a sketch map is not the determining piece of evidence that can assist the circumstances on how an accident happened. The Court of Appeal in the case of <a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2014/7990\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2014/7990\">Equator Distributors v Joel Muriu</a> [2018] eKLR pronounced itself as follows:</span><span class=\"akn-block\" data-name=\"quote\" id=\"arguments__para_18__block_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_18__block_1\"><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure\" data-startQuote=\"“\" id=\"arguments__para_18__block_1__embeddedStructure_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_18__block_1__embeddedStructure_1\"><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure--startQuote\">“</span><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure--content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_18__block_1__embeddedStructure_1__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_18__block_1__embeddedStructure_1__p_1\">On probative value to be given to a police sketch map, we are aware that a police sketch map for a road traffic accident is prepared after the event, it is not an eyewitness account. However, it carries some probative value. The sketch map is not binding on the trial court and it is for the trial court to establish facts from all the evidence on record. A police sketch map is just but an item of evidence to be considered.”</span></span></span></span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__para_19\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_19\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">19.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__para_19__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__para_19__p_1\">The appellant’s case was not challenged by any evidence to the contrary on a balance of probabilities, his case reached the threshold and the trial court fell into error by concluding otherwise. T is not surprising that the Respondent has not disputed the appellant’s case on liability in this appeal. He is found 100% liable.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-division akn--no-indent\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1\">\n<span class=\"akn-heading\">General damages</span><div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_20\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_20\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">20.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_20__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_20__p_1\">The trial court indicated that it suspected the Appellant because he could not receive treatment the same day then travelled to Mombasa and thereafter produced medical documents from Mutomo. Counsel has faulted the trial court on this fact and contended that the trial court erred in recording that the appellant received treatment from Mombasa. I went through the trial court’s handwritten record but unfortunately, the trial magistrate’s handwriting was not legible to me. In my view, I am inclined to accept the Appellant’s counsel submission that there was an error on the record as the Appellant produced treatment notes from Mutomo Hospital which indicate that he was attended to on 17<sup class=\"akn-sup\">th</sup> April 2018. This tallies with his testimony that he went to hospital on 17<sup class=\"akn-sup\">th</sup> April 2018 two days after the accident when he started experiencing pains.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">21.</span><span class=\"akn-intro\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__intro__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__intro__p_1\">Injuries recorded in the medical report tendered by Dr Titus Ndeti Nzina dated 28<sup class=\"akn-sup\">th</sup> May 2018 are as follows;</span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_i\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_i\"><span class=\"akn-num\">i.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_i__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_i__p_1\">Blunt injury to the chest</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_ii\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_ii\"><span class=\"akn-num\">ii.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_ii__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_ii__p_1\">Blunt injury to the lower back</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_iii\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_iii\"><span class=\"akn-num\">iii.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_iii__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_iii__p_1\">Blunt injury to the knees</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_iv\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_iv\"><span class=\"akn-num\">iv.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_iv__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_21__subpara_iv__p_1\">Blunt injury to the hip region.</span></span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">22.</span><span class=\"akn-intro\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__intro__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__intro__p_1\">The doctor’s findings on the appellant’s injury a little more than one month after the accident were;</span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_i\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_i\"><span class=\"akn-num\">i.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_i__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_i__p_1\">Tenderness on the anterior chest wall</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_ii\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_ii\"><span class=\"akn-num\">ii.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_ii__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_ii__p_1\">Tenderness on the lower back</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_iii\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_iii\"><span class=\"akn-num\">iii.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_iii__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_22__subpara_iii__p_1\">Tenderness on the hop region.</span></span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_23\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_23\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">23.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_23__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_23__p_1\">The trial court should have notwithstanding its finding on liability, assessed the damages it would have awarded it had found merit in the appellant’s case.</span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">24.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24__p_1\">The principles which ought to guide a court in awarding damages were set out by the Court of Appeal in <a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\">Southern Engineering Company Ltd. Vs. Musingi Mutia</a> [1985] KLR 730 where it was held that:</span><span class=\"akn-block\" data-name=\"quote\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24__block_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24__block_1\"><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure\" data-startQuote=\"“\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24__block_1__embeddedStructure_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24__block_1__embeddedStructure_1\"><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure--startQuote\">“</span><span class=\"akn-embeddedStructure--content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24__block_1__embeddedStructure_1__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_24__block_1__embeddedStructure_1__p_1\">It is trite law that the measurement of the quantum of damages is a matter for the discretion of the individual Judge, which of course has to be exercised judicially and with regard to the general conditions prevailing in the country generally, and to prior decisions which are relevant to the case in question.”</span></span></span></span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">25.</span><span class=\"akn-intro\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__intro__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__intro__p_1\">Comparative decisions on damages awarded for soft tissue injuries;</span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_a\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_a\"><span class=\"akn-num\">a.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_a__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_a__p_1\">In <a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\">G4s Security Services Ltd v Oyugi Obiria</a> [2018] eKLR, the court upheld an award of Kshs. 180,000/- awarded as general damages for pain, suffering and loss of amenities for the following injuries, blunt trauma to the neck- blunt-blunt injury to the back, tenderness to the right ear, blunt injury to anterior chest wall and swollen right hand.</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_b\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_b\"><span class=\"akn-num\">b.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_b__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_b__p_1\">In <a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\">Channan Agricultural Contractors Ltd v Fred Barasa Mutayo</a> [2013] eKLR , the High Court reviewed downwards an award of Kshs. 250,000/ to Kshs. 150,000/= for “moderate soft tissue injuries that were expected to heal in eight months’ time.</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_c\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_c\"><span class=\"akn-num\">c.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_c__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_c__p_1\">In <a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\">George Kinyanjui T/A Climax Coaches &amp; Anor. v Hussein Mahad Kuyale</a> [2016] Eklr, the High Court reviewed downwards an award of Kshs. 650,000/= to Kshs. 109,890/= for soft tissue injuries.</span></span></span><span class=\"akn-subparagraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_d\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_d\"><span class=\"akn-num\">d.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_d__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_25__subpara_d__p_1\">In <a data-href=\"/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\" target=\"_blank\" class=\"akn-ref\" href=\"http://resolver.caselaw.kenyalaw.org/resolver/akn/ke/judgment/kehc/2015/5180\">Dickson Ndungu Kirembe v Theresia Atieno &amp; 4 Others</a> [2014] eKLR the High Court reviewed downwards an award of Kshs. 255,000/= to Kshs. 127,500= for soft tissue injuries which healed with no complications.</span></span></span>\n</div>\n<div class=\"akn-paragraph\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_26\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_26\">\n<span class=\"akn-num\">26.</span><span class=\"akn-content\"><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_26__p_1\" data-eId=\"arguments__dvs_nn_1__para_26__p_1\">Going by the above authorities, this court finds and hereby awards the appellant kshs. 150.000/= in general damages. There was no document tendered to prove special damages other than an invoice from NTSA for kshs. 500/=. The claim for special damages therefore fails. The appellant will have costs and interests from the date of judgment in the trial court. He will also get costs of this appeal.</span></span>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div></div>\n<div id=\"conclusions\" class=\"akn-conclusions\">\n<span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"conclusions__p_1\" data-eId=\"conclusions__p_1\"><b class=\"akn-b\">DATED, SIGNED AND DELIVERED AT KITUI THIS 22<sup class=\"akn-sup\">ND</sup> DAY OF FEBRUARY, 2024</b></span><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"conclusions__p_2\" data-eId=\"conclusions__p_2\"><b class=\"akn-b\">HON. JUSTICE R. K. LIMO</b></span><span class=\"akn-p\" id=\"conclusions__p_3\" data-eId=\"conclusions__p_3\"><b class=\"akn-b\">JUDGE</b></span>\n</div></article></span>\n</div></div>\n\n\n
